- 2026-06-22 · mcp/query · **The unit test's fresh stores hid a live shared-store bug — multi-project only works if each project owns its store** (ama-74r/ama-mnj) — continuing the cross-project rollout, I threaded `projectPath` through all the session methods (the engine is now fully multi-project) and a batch of MCP tools (find_callers/callees/importers/imports, get_code_snippet, file_skeleton), and the unit test was green — index two fixture projects, route a query to each by path. Then the *live* dogfood failed in a way the test could not: `index_status` reported two registered projects with *identical* counts (the `xproj-a` entry showed Ama's 1652 nodes), and `find_callers`/`get_code_snippet` via `projectPath` returned Ama's data / null. Root cause: `Indexer.index()` calls `this.createStore()`, and the default `() => new InMemoryStore()` mints a *fresh* store per call — so the unit test's two projects are genuinely independent and it passes — but the live server reuses one store (a persisted/singleton), so every registry entry aliases the *last-indexed* project. The registry was holding N references to one mutating store. The deeper lesson is about the test itself: a unit test that constructs a fresh resource per instance *cannot* catch a bug that exists only because the real deployment *shares* that resource — its isolation is exactly what hides the aliasing. To test multi-instance behavior you must mirror the deployment's resource lifecycle, and absent that, the live dogfood is the only thing that catches it (which is why a re-index gate against the running server earns its keep). So the rollout reprioritized: threading the remaining ~14 tools is premature until each project gets an independent store (filed as the P2 blocker ama-mnj), and the navigation/read batch shipped is correct-but-blocked — right with isolated stores, wrong while they alias. Self-index 1651/4006/292 → 1652/4008/292 (+1 fixture symbol useAlpha for a meaningful cross-project find_callers). 571 → 572 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED by disabling projectPath routing; typecheck + repo-wide lint clean; live dogfood exposed the store-aliasing blocker.
- 2026-06-22 · indexer · **Parameterize the factory by the thing it's a factory *for* — the store-aliasing fix that made cross-project work live** (ama-mnj) — the blocker from last iteration: `Indexer`'s `createStore: () => Store` is a *zero-arg* factory, so a persistent factory (`() => new SqliteStore(fixedPath)`) hands every project the same db, aliasing them. A `() => Resource` factory bakes in "one resource"; the fix is to thread the discriminator — here the project root — so it becomes `(root) => Resource` and each project can get its own. Widened `createStore` to `(root: string) => Store`, passed `root` at the two call sites (`index`, `open`), and made the HTTP server persist *one* project to `AMA_DB` (the configured `AMA_ROOT`, or the first one indexed) while every other project gets its own `InMemoryStore`. The migration was cheap for a non-obvious TypeScript reason: *widening* a callback with an extra parameter is backward-compatible — a `() => Store` is assignable to `(root: string) => Store` (a function that ignores an argument still satisfies a type that supplies one), so the four single-project CLI factories (`() => new SqliteStore(dbPath)`) needed *no* change; only the one multi-project factory that actually needs the root did. TDD: a factory keyed by its arg (`Map<root, Store>`) yields two stores for two roots only if `index()` passes the root — RED (one shared store, `size === 1`) when the arg is dropped, GREEN once threaded. Dogfooded live, the exact failure from last iteration inverted: `find_callers("alpha", projectPath=xproj-a)` now returns `useAlpha` at `file: "a.ts"` (xproj-a's *own* path, not the Ama-relative one) and `get_code_snippet` returns the real source instead of null — the non-primary project is genuinely isolated. Lesson: a shared-resource aliasing bug is almost always a factory that closed over "the one resource" instead of taking a key; thread the key, and lean on the fact that adding a callback parameter doesn't break existing zero-arg callers. Self-index 1652/4008/292 → 1656/4020/293 (+1 test file). 572 → 573 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED by dropping the root arg; typecheck + repo-wide lint clean; live cross-project now returns isolated per-project data.
- 2026-06-22 · mcp · **`replace_all` is safe for *structurally* uniform code, not *incidentally* similar code — finishing the cross-project rollout** (ama-ibb) — with the store blocker fixed, I threaded `projectPath` through the last ~16 query tools so all 24 are cross-project. The tools split into two parts that look alike but aren't: the **handler plumbing** is structurally identical across every `{symbol}` tool (`({ symbol }: { symbol: string }) => session.findX(symbol)` — only `findX` varies), while the **schema** carries a per-tool human description (`"Method id…"`, `"Interface id…"`, `"Class id…"`, `"Handler symbol id…"`). I reached for `replace_all` on both. On the handlers it worked — the destructure and the `(symbol)),` call-tail are byte-identical everywhere, so two `replace_all`s threaded all of them at once. On the *schema* it silently under-matched: I matched only the common `"Symbol id…"` describe, so most tools got their handler threaded but **not** their schema. That mismatch is the dangerous kind — zod strips an unknown arg, so the tool would accept no `projectPath`, the handler would read `undefined`, and it'd quietly query the primary; `tsc` can't catch it because the handler's `projectPath` is optional. I reverted `server.ts` to the last commit and redid it deliberately: structural `replace_all`s for the describe-independent handler parts, one `replace_all` per *describe variant* for the schemas, then individual edits for the non-`{symbol}` tools (`{route}`, `{type}`, `impact`/`affected`/`explore`/`search_code` multi-arg, and the no-input `get_graph_schema`/`circular_imports` whose `{}` schema became `{ projectPath }`). The bug that `tsc` couldn't see is exactly what a **protocol-level** test catches: I added an MCP-client test that calls `impact_analysis` with `projectPath` and asserts it routes to the named project (RED — `[]` — when the handler drops the arg). Lesson: before a sweeping `replace_all`, separate what's *structurally* uniform (safe) from what's *incidentally* similar (a description that happens to repeat) — and when a wrapper's schema and handler can drift apart silently, test the wrapper through its real protocol, not just the function under it. Dogfooded live on the isolated non-primary project: `impact_analysis("alpha", projectPath=xproj-a)` → `[useAlpha]` and `get_graph_schema(projectPath=xproj-a)` → `{File:1, Function:2}` (xproj-a's tiny graph, not Ama's 1658). Self-index 1656/4020/293 → 1658/4024/293. 573 → 574 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED by dropping projectPath from a tool handler; typecheck + repo-wide lint clean. The cross-project surface (engine + all 24 tools + store isolation) is complete.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer · **A feature deferred for fear of "too noisy" was already bounded by an existing filter — measure, don't guess** (ama-emb) — the deep analyzer tracked `this.<prop>` reads as References (ama-qo3) but skipped cross-instance `obj.<prop>`, deferred because "every property access becomes a Reference" sounded explosive and might need an opt-in flag. The code change was one line — drop the `parent.expression.kind === ts.SyntaxKind.ThisKeyword` guard so *any* non-call `X.prop` read resolves. The real work was deciding whether that's safe, and the answer was already in the resolution path: `resolveValueRef → nodeIdForDecl` returns `undefined` for library code (anything `repoRel(root,…)` puts outside the repo) and for decls a structural walk wouldn't emit (locals, nested) — so `console.log`, `arr.map`, and every external/local member produce *no* edge. Dropping the guard therefore *cannot* create dangling edges or external noise; the blast radius is bounded to in-project top-level + class/interface members the graph actually has nodes for. Measured rather than guessed: on Ama's own source References went 452 → 811 (nearly 2×) but that's +9% of the whole graph (4024 → 4383 edges), call resolution was *unchanged* (3432/1177 — References aren't Calls, so the call graph is untouched), and every new edge targets a real node. Decision: keep it always-on, not opt-in — moderate, bounded growth buys a real capability. Dogfooded the payoff: `find_referrers("ProjectIndex.store")` was **empty** before (that field is only ever read as `project.store`, never `this.store`) and now returns the three methods that read it cross-instance (`close`, `indexStatus`, `register`) — exactly the "who uses this member" completeness the parent issue wanted. TDD: a fixture where `compare(other)` reads `other.value` and a free function `widen(b)` reads `b.size` — RED (referrers exclude `Box.compare`/`widen`) with the guard, GREEN without. Lesson: when a change is feared "too noisy," first check whether an existing resolution/emission filter already drops what would be noise — if it does, the feature is safe always-on, and a self-index measurement settles the growth question that a flag was about to paper over. Self-index 1658/4024/293 → 1661/4383/293 (+3 fixture nodes, +359 References edges). 574 → 575 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED by restoring the ThisKeyword guard; typecheck + repo-wide lint clean.
- 2026-06-22 · indexer · **The guard already existed — the gap was that it was *silent*, inconsistent with its sibling guard** (ama-j0y) — claimed "per-file parse timeout (guard against a pathological file hanging the index)" expecting to build a defense, but dogfooding the indexer first (`Indexer.index` → `discoverFiles`) showed the recommended tractable defense was *already there*: `discoverFiles` skips files over `MAX_FILE_SIZE_BYTES` (1 MB), and `find_callers(discoverFiles)` confirmed the cap is enforced consistently across `index`, `sync`, and (separately) the watcher — so a minified bundle / data blob never reaches the parser. The real gap wasn't the guard; it was that this skip was *silent* `continue`, while the sibling guard right below it (per-analyzer isolation) explicitly logs to stderr — its comment even says "reported to stderr (never silently dropped)." So two skip paths, one honest and one not. Fix matched the convention: an optional `onSkipLarge?` callback on `discoverFiles` (backward-compatible — `sync`'s call is untouched, no churn) and `index()` logs the skipped names to stderr, exactly like the isolation path (stdout stays JSON-RPC only). The true in-thread timeout for the *rarer* failure — a small file with catastrophic backtracking, under the size cap — genuinely needs a worker/subprocess and is disproportionate for the residual risk; filed as a low-priority follow-up. TDD: a temp dir with a >1 MB `huge.ts` + a normal `small.ts`, a `console.error` spy asserts `huge.ts` is named on stderr while `small.ts` still indexes — RED (silently dropped, spy never sees it), GREEN once reported. Lesson: when you pick up a "guard against X" item, first check whether the guard already exists — the actual gap is often not the mechanism but its *observability*, or its *consistency* with a sibling that already does it right; dogfooding the code before coding turned a "build a timeout" task into a one-callback honesty fix. Self-index 1661/4383/293 → 1662/4386/294 (+1 test file). 575 → 576 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED via a stderr spy before the skip was logged; typecheck + repo-wide lint clean.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer · **When a new shape doesn't fit a parameterized engine, a small self-contained analyzer behind the same interface beats contorting the engine** (ama-krw) — Vue/Svelte single-file components were indexed by *no* analyzer (`.vue`/`.svelte` routed nowhere). The instinct was to add a `.vue` `LanguageSpec` to the tree-sitter `BaselineAnalyzer`, but reading its `analyze` showed the mismatch: it parses *whole-file* content with one grammar, and an SFC is three languages in one file (`<template>` HTML + `<script>` JS/TS + `<style>` CSS) — feeding it to a JS grammar fails. Retrofitting a `preprocess`/offset hook onto the shared engine would touch every spec for one outlier. The `Analyzer` interface (`{language, tier, extensions, analyze}`) is the real seam: a self-contained `SfcAnalyzer` implementing it, registered alongside the others, needed *zero* changes to `BaselineAnalyzer` or the registry. It's parameterized by language (`new SfcAnalyzer("vue", [".vue"])`, `"svelte"`) so coverage reports them honestly and separately. Scoped to the chosen slice: a `Component` node named from the file + the `<script>` imports wired into the File→File import graph (deep `<script>` symbol/call analysis is a filed follow-up). Import resolution needed its *own* candidate set, not `jsCandidates` — an SFC imports composables/stores/sibling components, so `./helper` must try `.ts` and `./Card.vue` must be used as-is (jsCandidates only knows JS extensions and would append `.js` to `.vue`). Baseline-tier honesty: `<script>` import scanning is regex, a best-effort syntactic pass, not a full parse. Dogfooded live: `search_symbol("Widget")` now returns the `.vue` file's `Component` node, `find_imports(Widget.vue)` returns `helper.ts` + `Card.vue`, and `vue` shows up in the language census (baseline, 2 files). TDD: a `Widget.vue` fixture importing `./helper` (`.ts`) and `./Card.vue` — RED (no `Component` node, no Imports) with a stubbed-out resolver, GREEN once implemented. Lesson: the test for "does this belong in the shared engine?" is whether the new thing fits the engine's core assumption (here: one grammar per file). When it doesn't, implement the *interface*, not a special case inside the engine. Self-index 1662/4386/294 → 1682/4429/299 (+1 analyzer, +1 test, +3 fixtures; new `vue` coverage). 576 → 578 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED by neutering the import resolver; typecheck + repo-wide lint clean.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer · **A registered-but-untested capability is a claim without proof; and syntactic import scanning must enumerate the *forms*, not just the common one** (ama-grb) — ama-krw registered `SfcAnalyzer` for both `.vue` and `.svelte` but only tested `.vue`. Because the analyzer is language-agnostic (regex over the `<script>`), `.svelte` "just worked" — but "should work" isn't "is verified": a `.svelte` fixture + test turns a hope into a guarantee, and the Svelte detection + static-import assertions passed on the first run, locking the path in against future regression at no design cost. The actual new behavior was dynamic imports: SFCs lean on `import("./Foo.vue")` for lazy components/routes (code-splitting), and the scanner only matched `… from "x"` and bare `import "x"` — so every lazy import was invisible to the graph. Added a third pattern (`import\s*\(\s*['"]…`); it composes with the others without double-counting (`import(` has a paren, not a quote, so the bare-import regex never matched it). TDD made the split crisp: in one run, three assertions were GREEN (Svelte Component node, Svelte static import) — pure verification — and one was RED (the lazy `import("./Panel.svelte")` edge), the genuinely new work. Dogfooded live: `svelte` joins `vue` in the language census (baseline, 2 files each), and `find_imports("Toggle.svelte")` returns `helper.ts` (static) *and* `Panel.svelte` (dynamic). Lesson: when you register a capability for N inputs, test all N — the untested one is where a silent regression will land; and a syntactic import pass is only as complete as its enumeration of import *forms*, with the dynamic form the one most often forgotten and most load-bearing in modern component/route code. Self-index 1682/4429/299 → 1686/4433/301 (+2 Svelte fixtures; new `svelte` coverage). 578 → 580 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED on the dynamic-import edge before the third pattern; typecheck + repo-wide lint clean.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer · **Reusing an engine on a *fragment* means parameterizing it by an offset — and verify the refactor before the feature** (ama-q1u) — the deep SFC slice: make the `<script>`'s own declarations (functions, classes, methods, types) into nodes, not just the file's Component + imports. Two enablers fell into place. First, `tree-sitter-typescript.wasm` was already in the `tree-sitter-wasms` bundle (alongside `tsx`/`vue`), so adding `typescript` to the grammar registry unblocked parsing `<script lang="ts">` (the TS grammar parses plain JS too, so one grammar covers every SFC script). Second — the load-bearing design move — the `BaselineAnalyzer`'s symbol walk was a *private method* tied to whole-file parsing; I extracted it to a shared `walkSymbols(…, lineOffset = 0)` so the SFC analyzer can run the *same* walk on a `<script>` parsed out of the middle of a file, with `lineOffset` mapping every emitted line back to the real file. The offset is the crux: tree-sitter reports positions relative to the string it parsed (the extracted `<script>` body), so a symbol at script-row R lands at file line `R + 1 + (lines before the block's content)` — verified by asserting `greet`'s `startLine === 12` (RED at 7 without the offset; off-by-one is the default bug here, so the test pins the exact line). Two process lessons banked. (1) Verify a refactor *in isolation*: after extracting `walkSymbols` I ran the baseline suite (33/33) *before* wiring SFC, so a failure would unambiguously be the extraction, not the new feature — separating the two risk surfaces. (2) sync→async ripples to callers: making `analyze` async (parse is async) silently turned the test's `const result = analyze(...)` into a Promise and broke all five SFC tests at once; the fix (`beforeAll(async … await)`) is a reminder that changing a return type is an interface change. Dogfooded live: `get_code_snippet("tests/fixtures/sfc/Widget.vue#greet")` returns the function body at lines 12–14 — read straight out of the `.vue` because the offset is right. Calls/types inside `<script>` stay deep-tier (out of scope). Self-index 1686/4433/301 → 1689/4447/302 (+1 file: the shared `walk.ts`; new SFC `<script>` symbols). 580 → 581 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED by zeroing the line offset; typecheck + repo-wide lint clean.
- 2026-06-22 · sidecar · **Define and validate a cross-process *contract* first — independent of either implementation** (ama-3bb.1) — the first slice of the deep-tier sidecar epic (the project's core thesis: .NET/Java analyzed deeply by out-of-process helpers). Rather than start on a .NET or Java sidecar, I built the protocol they'll both speak: NDJSON over stdio (Ama writes `analyze` requests to the subprocess's stdin, reads `ready`/`result`/`error` messages from its stdout; stderr is logs only — the same "stdout is JSON-only" rule Ama's own MCP server lives by), everything `tier: "deep"`, with zod schemas that *validate on the way in* so a buggy sidecar can't inject an ill-formed node/edge into the graph (proved by a test that rejects an unknown node `kind` — RED when the kind schema is loosened to `z.string()`). Two things made the contract honest. (1) It reuses the graph model as its wire shapes, and the round-trip test frames a *typed* `GraphNode`/`GraphEdge` literal — so if the model grows a required field, the test literal stops compiling and the wire schema is forced to follow; the boundary can't silently drift from the model. (2) The validator needs the kinds at runtime, but `EdgeKind` was a hand-written union with no array (unlike `NODE_KINDS`), so I mirrored the pattern with an `EDGE_KINDS` array and derived `EdgeKind` from it — a single source of truth the type *and* the validator share, motivated by (and now enabling) the protocol. Scoped deliberately to the contract — schemas + framing (`frame`/`parseRequest`/`parseMessage`) + the spec doc, no subprocess; the TS harness (`SidecarAnalyzer` that spawns and speaks it) and the real .NET/Java sidecars are filed follow-ups that all build against this proven spec. Lesson: at a process boundary, the contract is the artifact to nail first — a validated, round-trippable spec lets both ends develop independently against something already known-correct, and a trust boundary (untrusted subprocess output) earns strict enum validation backed by a single source of truth. Self-index 1689/4447/302 → 1710/4493/304 (+2 files: the protocol module + its test). 581 → 586 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED by loosening node-kind validation; EDGE_KINDS refactor type-checked clean across the codebase; repo-wide lint clean.
- 2026-06-22 · sidecar · **To test a cross-process boundary, mock the other *process*, not the IPC mechanism** (ama-h9t) — the harness that makes the ama-3bb.1 protocol executable: a `SidecarAnalyzer` implementing `Analyzer` (tier `deep`) that spawns a subprocess, writes a framed `analyze` request to its stdin, reads NDJSON from its stdout (`readline` → `parseMessage`), resolves on the matching `result`, and tears the process down. The sidecar's stderr is inherited so its logs reach Ama's stderr, never the protocol stream; a spawn failure / malformed line / `error` reply / exit-without-result all reject, and the indexer's per-analyzer isolation then skips that language (graceful baseline fall-through is the routing layer, ama-3bb.4). The key testing move: I proved it against a ~20-line **mock sidecar** — a standalone `.mjs` that emits `ready` then a `result` with one File node per requested file — rather than mocking `child_process` or stubbing the stream. That exercises the *real* spawn + stdio + NDJSON-framing path end-to-end, so the test covers the actual integration the .NET/Java sidecars will use; a mocked-IPC test would pass without ever spawning anything or round-tripping a byte. TDD: the round-trip returns the mock's deep nodes, and a bogus command rejects — RED proven by skipping the request write (the mock then exits without a result → reject). The protocol + harness are now the complete TS-side foundation; a real sidecar just has to emit the protocol on stdout. (Not yet registered in the indexer — wiring + deep-if-available routing is ama-3bb.4.) Lesson: when the thing under test is a boundary between processes, the highest-fidelity test drives a real process on the other side speaking the real wire format; mocking the transport tests your mock, not the integration. Self-index 1710/4493/304 → 1723/4522/307 (+3 files: the harness, its test, the mock sidecar). 586 → 588 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED by skipping the request send; typecheck + repo-wide lint clean. - 2026-06-22 · sidecar · **"Deep-if-available, else baseline" is two orthogonal rules — split them and each tests in isolation** (ama-3bb.4) — the routing decision for the sidecar epic decomposed cleanly into (a) *deep beats baseline* for a shared extension and (b) *an unavailable analyzer isn't in the running*, and keeping them separate made each trivially testable and the combined behavior fall out with no special-casing. (a) lives in the registry: `register` now keeps the deep analyzer when an extension is claimed by both, *regardless of registration order* — the order-independence is the subtle part (the RED: revert to last-wins and a deep-registered-first analyzer loses to a later baseline). (b) is an optional `Analyzer.isAvailable?()` (in-process analyzers omit it ⇒ always available) plus `registry.registerIfAvailable`, which probes and only registers if the probe passes; `SidecarAnalyzer.isAvailable()` spawns its subprocess and waits for the `ready` handshake within a timeout (spawn failure / early exit / timeout ⇒ unavailable). Compose them: an available deep sidecar registers and wins; an unavailable one never registers, so the baseline — the only analyzer left for that extension — keeps the language. Tested end-to-end with the real mock sidecar (available ⇒ `forFile(".cs")` is deep; a bogus command ⇒ baseline) and the handshake probe (mock ⇒ true, bogus ⇒ false). "Report which tier produced each result" — the other half of the bead — was already true (every analyzer declares its tier and every node carries it). Not wired into `createDefaultIndexer` yet: there is no real sidecar to register (that's ama-3bb.2/.3, which call `registerIfAvailable`), and Ama's own analyzers share no extensions, so this is a no-op on its own index — the mechanism is complete and proven, awaiting a real sidecar. Lesson: when a policy reads as "X-if-condition, else Y," look for the two independent rules hiding inside it; testing "X beats Y" and "condition gates X" separately is far cleaner than testing the four-way combined outcome. Self-index 1723/4522/307 → 1731/4546/308 (+1 file: the routing test). 588 → 592 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED by reverting the registry to last-wins; typecheck + repo-wide lint clean. Sidecar epic now 3/5 — the TS side (protocol + harness + routing) is complete; only the real .NET (ama-3bb.2) and Java (ama-3bb.3) sidecars remain, and those need a .NET/JVM toolchain.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer · **To test a precision win over a recall-favoring heuristic, construct the ambiguity the heuristic gets wrong — and generalize before the third copy** (ama-66z) — C# `using` resolution used an ancestor-scan + shortening-suffix heuristic (favoring recall); the precision gap was that a *coincidental* directory at a closer ancestor could win over the real one. The fix: parse the nearest `.csproj`'s `<RootNamespace>` (default: the project file name) and, when a `using` is under it, map the namespace *exactly* to that project's directory tree as a precise first attempt, falling back to the suffix-scan. The subtle testing point: the heuristic already nails the conventional layout, so a precision improvement is invisible unless the test builds the case the heuristic mis-handles — here a fixture with a decoy `Deep/Sub/Wrong.cs` next to the importer and the real `Sub/Real.cs` at the project root, where the suffix-scan grabs the closer decoy (RED) but RootNamespace maps `App.Sub` to the exact `Sub/` (GREEN). The other half was a consolidation: the `.csproj` walk-up is the *third* instance of "find the nearest project config walking up, parse it, cache it" (after Go's `go.mod` and PHP's `composer.json`), whose `parentDir` was byte-identical and whose walk+cache differed only in the parse — so rather than copy a third time, I extracted a generic `nearestConfig<T>(root, dir, read, cache)` (a `read(absDir)` callback supplies the per-language parse) and refactored all three onto it, verifying Go/PHP stayed green *before* adding C#. Dogfooded: `find_imports` on the decoy fixture's importer returns only `Sub/Real.cs`. Lesson: a recall-favoring heuristic's imprecision only bites in the ambiguous case, so the test must manufacture that ambiguity; and the moment a pattern is about to have a third copy, generalize the two you have (callback out the part that varies) — the project keeps proving that the chokepoint is cheaper than the copies. Self-index 1731/4546/308 → 1745/4566/312 (+4 files: the shared config helper + the C# `.csproj` fixture). 592 → 593 tests; full suite + self-index gate green; RED by disabling the precise `.csproj` attempt; Go/PHP refactor verified green in isolation; typecheck + repo-wide lint clean.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer · **A feature for one *form* of a read leaves the other forms as inconsistencies — cover every form, or the query is incomplete** (ama-eda) — with the clean backlog thin, I dogfooded for a gap: `ama-emb` made `obj.<prop>` reads `References`, so I asked whether *destructuring* the same property is tracked. `find_referrers("IndexStats.fileCount")` returned only `http.ts` (which writes `stats.fileCount`) and **missed** `cli/commands/status.ts`, which does `const { …, fileCount } = opened.stats` — a read of the exact same member, invisible because it's a binding pattern, not a `.prop` expression. So "who reads this property" was only half-answered. - 2026-06-22 · process · **At a maturity plateau, consolidate delivered work and record decisions — don't manufacture marginal features** (ama-5gs/ama-m8k.6/ama-da3) — after ~18 iterations the *actionable, in-stack* backlog is genuinely exhausted: a fresh `bd ready` sweep showed the CLI epic at 9/9 (bd flags it "eligible for close"), cross-project delivered, and every remaining item blocked (Ruby grammar won't load), out-of-stack (the .NET/Java sidecars need a `dotnet`/JVM toolchain this environment lacks), platform-hard (Linux recursive watch), or a self-described skip (type-param nodes). - 2026-06-22 · cli/release · **An install entry to an existing server is free when the server's launcher already supervises stdio** (ama-fmu, release 0.1) — coding agents spawn a local MCP server as `{ command, args }` over stdio, but the installed `ama` bin had no way to *start* the server (only `node dist/mcp/server.js`). Adding `ama mcp` turned out to be three lines: in the CLI's `main()`, `if (argv[0] === "mcp") { const { main } = await import("../mcp/server.js"); await main(); return; }` — no `process.exit`, because the stdio transport owns the lifecycle (it stays alive until the client disconnects). It needed *no* new server plumbing because the WASM-tier guard (`ensureBaselineWasmTier`) already re-execs with `stdio: "inherit"`, so a spawned `ama mcp` pipes the JSON-RPC channel straight through to the pinned child — the exact mechanism the dev `node dist/mcp/server.js` config relies on. Tested at the highest fidelity: spawn the *real* CLI subprocess via the MCP SDK's `StdioClientTransport`, do a real `initialize` + `listTools`, and assert `index_repository`/`search_symbol` are advertised — with `AMA_WASM_TIER_REEXEC=1` to skip the re-exec for a deterministic single-process spawn (RED proven by disabling the route → the process exits as an unknown command → "Connection closed"). Lesson: when bolting a CLI/install entry onto an existing server, route to its existing `main()` rather than re-implementing transport, and prove the entry by driving the real binary through the real client over the real transport — not a mock. First slice of the 0.1 release (ama-kgw). 594 → 595 tests; full suite + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · release · **Making a `private` repo publishable is a checklist, and `npm pack --dry-run` is the cheap proof — plus: any version literal not read from package.json drifts** (ama-0rj) — to ship `@mka-rainmaker/ama@0.1.0`: drop `private: true`; use a scoped name (the unscoped `ama` is taken) with `publishConfig.access: public` so `npm publish` goes public without the `--access` flag; whitelist `files: ["dist","README.md","LICENSE"]` (npm packs *that*, not `.gitignore` — `dist/` is gitignored yet still ships); add `prepublishOnly: "npm run build && npm test"` so `dist/` is built and the suite gates every publish. Verified with `npm pack --dry-run` — 203 files, all `dist/**` plus the three roots, *no* `src`/`tests`/`.beads` — confirming the surface rather than trusting the config. Version single-source: `build-info.ts` already derives `serverStamp.version` from package.json, but `server.ts` hard-coded the MCP-handshake `version: "0.0.1"` — pointed it at `serverStamp.version` so the bump propagates from one place (`ama --version` → 0.1.0; the version-stamp test stayed green). Lesson: a publishable-package config should be *proven* with a dry-run pack, and a version string that isn't read from the manifest will silently lie. Release epic ama-kgw now 2/5 (mcp entry + publishable package). 595 tests green; build + typecheck + lint clean.
- 2026-06-22 · release · **Battle-testing the *installed* package caught a release-breaker no source test could — the symlinked-bin no-op** (ama-8fa, ama-3to) — `npm pack` → install the tarball into a clean temp dir → `ama --version`, and the installed bin printed *nothing* (exit 0, only a stderr warning). Cause: npm installs a `bin` as a symlink (`.bin/ama → dist/cli/index.js`), so `process.argv[1]` is the *symlink* path while `import.meta.url` is the *resolved* file — the entry guard `process.argv[1] === fileURLToPath(import.meta.url)` is false, so `main()` never ran. Every test of the *source* passed, because they invoke the real file directly (`argv[1]` === the file); only an install-from-tarball run reaches the symlinked shape. Fix: an `isEntrypoint(importMetaUrl)` helper comparing *resolved realpaths* (`realpathSync` both sides), used by all three entries (cli, server, http); unit-tested with a real temp symlink (RED = raw equality). After the fix the full battle-test passed: the installed `ama mcp` indexed the Ama repo to **1754/4608/315 across all 14 languages** (deep TS + 13 baseline) and `search_symbol`/`find_callers` returned correct results — proving deps + tree-sitter grammars/wasm resolve from a real `npm install`, not just the dev `node_modules`. Lesson: a package's tests run against the source tree where the bin *is* the real file; the installed shape (symlinked bin, deps from a fresh install) is a different world, and `npm pack` → temp-install → smoke is the cheapest catch for "works in the repo, broken on install." Release epic ama-kgw 3/5 (mcp entry + publishable package + battle-test/bin-fix). 595 → 598 tests; the package is install-proven.
- 2026-06-22 · release/docs · **A release README must be re-grounded against what actually ships, and a stdio MCP entry makes cross-client install docs near-identical** (ama-5r6) — the pre-release README still read "Status: **early** … roadmap mostly ahead of us," with every roadmap box unchecked and `explore`/`impact_analysis` tagged "(planned)" — while the product had shipped deep TS + 13 baseline languages, 27 tools, SQLite persistence, cross-project queries, and a CLI. Aspirational docs that lag the code mislead the first users at the worst possible moment. Rewrote to a "0.1 / What's in 0.1 / Next" framing and added an Install section. Notably the per-agent setup collapsed to *one* shape: Claude Code, Cursor, Windsurf, and any other MCP client all spawn `{ "command": "ama", "args": ["mcp"] }` over stdio — only the config file path differs — so "configure your agent" is one command (`claude mcp add ama -- ama mcp`) plus one JSON block, not four bespoke integrations. Left the repo's `.mcp.json` as-is: it's the *dev* config (HTTP + `tsx watch` for live reload during the self-improvement loop), deliberately different from the install form. Lesson: at release, audit the README against reality (counts, tiers, the actual tool list) the way you'd audit code; and because every MCP client takes the same `{ command, args }` stdio block, documenting one install covers them all. Release epic ama-kgw 4/5 — only `npm publish` (the maintainer's step) remains. No code change (README only); 598 tests still green.
- 2026-06-22 · runtime/debugging · **An experimental-feature warning on a built-in fires during ESM *linking* for a static import — to filter it you must install the filter, then load the module at runtime** (ama-hee) — `node:sqlite` prints `ExperimentalWarning: SQLite is an experimental feature` on every run. First fix attempt — override `process.emitWarning` via a side-effect import placed *before* `import "node:sqlite"` in `store/sqlite.ts` — failed: the warning still printed (twice — parent + the wasm-tier re-exec child). Root cause, proven by a 2-file experiment (`import "./q.mjs"` sets the override, then `import "node:sqlite"`): the warning prints **before** the override module's body runs. ESM links the whole graph (loading the builtin → emitting the warning) in a phase that **precedes** evaluation (where any module body, including the override, executes); so no import *ordering* can install the override in time for a static import. Two more experiments confirmed the cure: `override; await import("node:sqlite")` **and** `override; createRequire(...)("node:sqlite")` both suppress it, because a runtime load links node:sqlite when the call executes — after the override is in place. Chose `createRequire` (synchronous) over dynamic import to avoid turning `sqlite.ts` into a top-level-await async module that would ripple through every importer. Final shape: `import type * as NodeSqlite from "node:sqlite"` for types (erased at runtime, no load), the filter installed via a static side-effect import, then `const { DatabaseSync } = createRequire(import.meta.url)("node:sqlite") as typeof NodeSqlite` in the body. Bonus gotcha: biome wrapped an inline `as typeof import("node:sqlite")` with a trailing comma → a TS parse error that broke 22 test files' *loads* (22 files failed, only "2 tests failed" — the tell that it's a load error, not logic); the namespace-type-import form is both cleaner and biome-stable. Verified end-to-end: reinstalled tarball → `ama --version 2>&1` prints only `0.1.0`. Lesson: for a built-in's link-time warning, ordering static imports is futile — install the filter, then `require`/dynamic-`import` the module. 598 → 601 tests; suite + typecheck + lint green.

- 2026-06-22 · validation/coverage · **Battle-testing 0.1 on a large real Next.js repo: clean scale, one framework-coverage gap, one harness self-own** (ama-vzq) — drove the published `ama mcp` against a large real Next.js + TypeScript codebase (2222 source files): indexed in **~6s to 18276 nodes / 34183 edges**, no OOM or crash (the 68G on disk is gitignored node_modules/.git — discovery prunes it and indexes only the ~2222 analyzable files). `search_symbol`/`get_code_snippet`/`find_referrers`/`impact_analysis`/`find_routes` all worked when called correctly. **Gap:** 0 Route nodes — Ama's filename-based routing covers the Next *Pages* router (+ Astro/Nuxt) but not the *App* router (`src/app/**/page.tsx`, `route.ts`), now the Next default, so `find_routes`/`find_handlers` silently no-op there (filed ama-vzq). **Harness self-own worth remembering:** calling `find_routes` with `{}` returned `MCP error -32602: … "symbol" Required` — I first misread it as a tool bug; the tool was fine (it maps a handler *symbol* → routes), my call was wrong. Two lessons: (1) battle-testing real third-party repos surfaces framework-coverage gaps that self-indexing never can — Ama's own repo has no routes, so only a real app exposed the App-Router gap; (2) "tool errored" in a battle-test is as often a harness/contract mistake as a real defect — read the error (a -32602 validation error is *your* bad input, not the server's bug) before filing. Scale validated on real code; App-Router support filed as ama-vzq.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer/framework · **Next.js App Router support was a one-branch addition because the routing infra already did the hard parts** (ama-vzq) — the gap looked framework-sized but wasn't. Dogfooding the route helpers via Ama's own tools (`get_code_snippet`/`find_callers` on `filenameRoutePath`/`fileRoutePath`/`routeSegment`) showed the infrastructure was already most of the way to App Router: `routeSegment` already maps `[id]`→`:id`, `[...slug]`→`*`, `[[opt]]`→`:opt` and drops `(group)`; `fileRoutePath` already detects `app/**/route.ts` (named GET/POST handlers). The *only* missing piece was the dominant App Router file — `page.tsx` (a default-export page component). Fix: in `filenameRoutePath`, add `if (appIdx >= 0 && base === "page") { rootIdx = appIdx; allowDefault = true; dropFilename = true }`, and drop the `page` filename from the path (like `index`) so it maps to its directory; the existing `allowDefault` path then links the default export as the `ALL <path>` handler. **Guard worth remembering:** dropped the filename via a `dropFilename` flag set *only* in the app branch — NOT by adding `"page"` to the global `base === "index"` leaf check, which would wrongly collapse a non-app `pages/page.tsx` to `/`. TDD: `app/dashboard/page.tsx`, `app/blog/[slug]/page.tsx`, `app/(marketing)/pricing/page.tsx`, `app/page.tsx` (+ `app/layout.tsx` as a negative) → RED (0 app routes) → GREEN. Real-world proof: re-indexing the App Router repo that exposed the gap went **0 → 70 routes**, with correct dynamic segments (`/:brandId/content/:itemId`) and elided groups. Lesson: a "framework not supported" gap is often far smaller than it looks — read the existing helpers before assuming a big build; here a reusable segment grammar + marker detection left a single conditional. 601 → 606 tests; suite + self-index + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer/prisma · **A Prisma schema is cheap to graph: a ~150-line line parser, no tree-sitter, reusing existing node/edge kinds** (ama-cdg, first slice of Prisma-aware impact analysis) — `schema.prisma` is a flat declarative grammar (`model`/`enum`/`type` blocks; `name Type @attrs` fields), so a brace-tracked line parser handles it — *no wasm grammar to source/bundle*, which was the very thing that made generic SQL expensive. Choices that kept blast radius tiny: models → the existing `Class` kind, enums → `Enum` (no `NODE_KINDS` churn — and a grep confirmed there are no exhaustive `kind` switches to break before relying on that); relations → the existing `UsesType` edge (a field typed by another declared model/enum emits `from-model UsesType target`). Two-pass — collect every declared type name + its file first, then parse — so a relation links across a multi-file `schema/` dir, not just one file. `datasource`/`generator` blocks are skipped via brace-depth; per-file try/catch isolates a malformed schema. TDD: a fixture (2 models + a both-directions relation + an enum + datasource/generator) → 5 cases RED→GREEN. Real-world: indexing the motivating Prisma repo added `prisma:deep(1)` and **92 model nodes** (+ ~54 enums). This is the *substrate* — the payoff lands in the next child (link `prisma.<model>` calls in TS → these nodes) where find_referrers/impact_analysis cross the code↔schema boundary. Lesson: when the source is a clean declarative grammar, a line parser + reused graph kinds beats reaching for a grammar or a new node kind — ship the substrate, defer the kind/grammar polish. 606 → 611 tests; suite + self-index + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer/prisma · **Cross-analyzer linkage (code↔schema) is the dispatch pattern: a name-keyed candidate edge + a whole-graph resolve pass** (ama-kvv — the Prisma impact-analysis payoff) — `prisma.<model>` in TS now links to the schema model node, so find_referrers/impact_analysis cross the code↔schema boundary. Two analyzers can't see each other's nodes at `analyze()` time, so — exactly like `deriveDispatchEdges` for interface dispatch — the TS analyzer emits a *raw candidate*: a References edge whose target encodes the model **name** (`prisma:model:<prop>`, provenance `prisma-ref`), and a whole-graph pass (`derivePrismaReferences`, run via `relinkPrisma` after every index/reindex like `redispatch`) resolves it to the model node by name, **dropping any candidate that matches no model**. That drop is the safety net: detection is deliberately loose (a name-based `isPrismaClientExpr`: base ∈ {prisma,db,tx,…}), so `prisma.$transaction`, `db.connect`, an HTTP `client.users` emit candidates that simply don't resolve. The TS hook was *free* — `prisma.user` already lands in `collectVarReferences`' `X.prop` branch (the member-read path), so I just branch on `isPrismaClientExpr(parent.expression)` before the normal `resolveValueRef`. Ripple worth noting: adding `prisma`/`prisma-ref` to the `EdgeProvenance` union broke an exhaustive provenance census in `getGraphSchema` *and* one exact-match test — typecheck and the suite caught both (the payoff of an enumerated provenance type). Real-world on the motivating repo: **620 resolved code→model links across 92 models** (977 candidates, the rest `$transaction`/`$queryRaw`/non-models, self-dropped); `find_referrers("ai_provider_configs")` → 10 referrers, e.g. `countEnabledProviders` in `ai-provider.repository.ts`. Candidates persist so incremental reindex re-resolves whole-graph (renames included); they're invisible to `find_referrers(model)` (wrong target). Lesson: cross-analyzer edges follow the dispatch playbook — emit a name-keyed candidate, resolve whole-graph, let non-matches self-drop. 611 → 614 tests; suite + self-index + typecheck + lint green. Epic ama-apy 2/4 (parser + linkage = the MVP).

The CLI passes `os.homedir()`; tests pass a temp dir. - 2026-06-22 · mcp/parity · **Gating N tool registrations: one bind+cast wrapper beats N guards — and keep the default non-breaking + the env a parameter** (ama-tqm, competitive-parity) — a comparable tool defaults to *one* tool (`explore`) with the rest opt-in; Ama's parity is `AMA_MCP_TOOLS` (unset → all 27, **non-breaking**; `minimal` → `explore` + the index bootstrap; or a comma-list). Rather than 27 `if` guards or a data-driven refactor of the registrations, wrap `server.registerTool` once: (1) **bind it first** — `const baseRegister = server.registerTool.bind(server)` — so a `replace_all "server.registerTool(" → "tool("` rewrites the 27 call sites but *not* the wrapper (the `.bind(` has no `(` straight after `registerTool`), avoiding self-recursion; (2) **cast the wrapper `as typeof server.registerTool`** so the 27 call sites keep full handler-type inference (the impl params are loose `any`, the public type is the cast); (3) `reg.disable()` the non-selected — `disable()` hides a tool from `tools/list` (confirmed, no need for `remove()`). Testability: `createServer(session, toolsSpec = process.env.AMA_MCP_TOOLS)` — tests pass `"minimal"` directly, no env mutation. Verified end-to-end: `AMA_MCP_TOOLS=minimal` over a spawned `ama mcp` lists exactly `explore/index_repository/index_status`. Lesson: to conditionally gate many call sites of a *generic* SDK method, a single bind-then-cast wrapper is the clean lever — bind dodges rename-recursion, the cast preserves caller inference — and a feature like this stays safe by defaulting to the old behavior and reading its config via a parameter, not `process.env` deep inside. competitive-parity epic ama-514 2/6. 625 → 630 tests; suite + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer/parity · **Baseline route detection is a new optional `LanguageSpec` hook; Python Flask/FastAPI is the first language — and TS framework breadth was already done** (ama-bvg) — surveying first corrected a wrong assumption (NestJS + Express/Fastify/Hapi/Koa/Hono/tRPC/GraphQL + the filename routers are *all* already covered in the deep tier; I nearly rebuilt Nest — see the verify-before-build note). The real gap is *baseline-language* frameworks, so the baseline (tree-sitter) tier gained route detection: an optional `LanguageSpec.collectRoutes(root, rel) → {nodes, edges}`, called in `BaselineAnalyzer.analyze` right beside `collectImports` (mirroring the existing import-resolver hook, so the new capability cost ~one field + one call). Python implements it: a decorated function whose decorator is `@<obj>.route(path)` (Flask, GET default) or `@<obj>.<verb>(path)` (FastAPI/Flask) becomes a `METHOD /path` Route → the function. Two details that mattered: (1) **normalize path params across frameworks to one form** — Flask `<id>`/`<int:id>` and FastAPI `{id}` both → `:id` — so baseline routes read uniformly with the TS routers' `:id`; (2) the handler edge's target must match `walkSymbols`' nesting, so `qualifiedNameOf` walks the function's class/function ancestors (top-level def → its name; class method → `Class.method`) — get this wrong and the route dangles to a non-node. The hook is now the template for the rest: Go (`r.GET` call-reg), Rust (`#[get]` attribute), PHP/Java/Django each add their own `collectRoutes`. Lesson: a cross-cutting baseline-tier capability is cheap when modeled as an optional per-spec hook like the import resolver, and a shared route-param normalization keeps multi-framework routes queryable the same way. ama-bvg: TS frameworks (already) + Python (this) done; remaining languages filed as a follow-up. Parity epic ama-514 3/6. 630 → 633 tests; suite + self-index + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · cli/parity · **Codex in `ama install`: a `format` discriminator on the agent spec + a text-based TOML table merge, no parser dependency** (ama-p6h) — the installer was JSON-only; Codex stores MCP servers in `~/.codex/config.toml` as `[mcp_servers.ama]` (command/args, TOML). Rather than add a TOML-parser dependency for a single known table, the merge is **text-based**: `removeAmaToml` splices the `[mcp_servers.ama]` table (its header through the line before the next `^[` table header, or EOF), and `withAmaServerToml` strips-then-appends a canonical table — idempotent, other tables untouched. `AgentSpec` gained `format: "json" | "toml"`; `installAgents`/`uninstallAgents` branch on it (`writeJson`+`withAmaServer` vs `writeText`+`withAmaServerToml`), and the "is ama already present" check is per-format too (the `mcpServers.ama` key vs the `[mcp_servers.ama]` header substring). TDD: pure TOML merge (append / idempotent / remove-only-ama) + a temp-`$HOME` Codex install+uninstall integration. Real-world: the dev-build `ama install --dry-run` now detects **both** Claude Code and Codex on this machine. Lesson: to edit a single known table in a config format you don't have a parser for, a header→next-header text splice is enough and avoids a dependency; a `format` discriminator on the spec keeps the installer open to the next format (Codex now, others later) without touching the JSON path. (This also makes the Codex wiring I'd hand-appended earlier idempotent + automatic.) competitive-parity epic ama-514 — installer follow-up. 633 → 638 tests; suite + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · benchmarks/honesty · **A benchmark's credibility lives in its baseline assumption and its framing — so state both** (ama-foz) — a comparable tool's headline is a measured *whole-agent* savings; a comparable Ama number needs an LLM loop (deferred). What I built instead is a deterministic, reproducible *retrieval-efficiency* proxy (`scripts/benchmark.mjs`): per real code question, one Ama MCP call + its result tokens vs. the no-graph baseline (grep the symbol, read every matching file in full). On Ama's own repo: **5 calls / 13.2K tok vs 129 calls / 1.38M tok → ~99% fewer tokens, ~96% fewer tool calls.** The number is real but the *baseline* drives it: reading every grep-matching file in full is a thorough-agent **upper bound** (a leaner agent reads fewer), so 99% is a ceiling, not a promise — `docs/benchmarks/README.md` says so loudly and frames it as retrieval efficiency, explicitly *not* the full-agent-cost figure a comparable tool's % is. The temptation with a benchmark is to publish the big number; the honest move is to publish the number *with its baseline and its limits*, and to name the part a token count understates (Ama's answer is precise + structured — real callers, transitive impact, no grep false positives). Lesson: present a proxy as a proxy; a benchmark you'd defend states what it measures, what it assumes, and what it doesn't. No product-code change (a script + docs); 638 tests unaffected. competitive-parity epic ama-514 4/6.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer/parity · **The `collectRoutes` hook generalizes from decorator-walk to call-walk: Go (Gin/chi) routing** (ama-a2r) — Python's detector walked *decorated functions*; Go registers routes by *call* (`r.GET("/users/:id", getUser)`), so `goRoutes` walks `call_expression`s whose `function` is a `selector_expression` with an HTTP-verb `field` and a string first arg → a `METHOD /path` Route, with a best-effort handler edge when the 2nd arg is a plain identifier (resolves when the handler is a top-level func in the same file). Two reuse wins: the `:param` normalization is shared in spirit (chi `{id}` → `:id`; Gin `:id` is already the target form), keeping Go routes uniform with the TS/Python ones; and `goStringArg` strips quotes exactly like the existing `goImports`, so the file stayed internally consistent. Confirmed via Ama's own tools before coding (get_code_snippet on `goSpec` + `pythonRoutes`) — dogfooding surfaced that `goImports` already had the string-strip idiom to mirror. Lesson: a per-spec `collectRoutes` hook absorbs *structurally different* framework idioms (decorator vs call vs — next — annotation/attribute) behind one interface; each language only answers "which CST shape registers a route?" ama-a2r: Go done; Rust/PHP/Java/Django remain. 638 → 640 tests; suite + self-index + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer/parity · **`ama-a2r` complete — one `collectRoutes` hook absorbed four more framework idioms; the lever was dumping each CST before writing the detector.** After Go (call-registration), the remaining baseline-language routers all landed on the same hook, each a *structurally different* CST shape: **PHP** Laravel (`Route::get` — a `scoped_call_expression`, scoped to the `Route` facade so `Cache::get` doesn't match), **Java** Spring (class `@RequestMapping` prefix + method `@GetMapping`/`@PostMapping` annotations → `Class.method` handler), **Rust** actix (`#[get("/x")]` — an `attribute_item` that's a preceding *sibling* of the `function_item`, not a child), **Django** (`urls.py` `path()`/`re_path()` calls — method-agnostic, so `ANY /path`; extends `python.ts` alongside the Flask/FastAPI decorators). The technique that made four unfamiliar grammars cheap and first-try-correct: a throwaway AST dumper (`_dump.ts` — parse a fixture, print named node types) run per language to **confirm** the CST before coding — e.g. it revealed Rust's attribute is a preceding sibling and Java's annotation arg nests `annotation → annotation_argument_list → string_literal → string_fragment` — instead of guess-and-debug. Shared in spirit across all five: normalize `{id}`/`<int:id>` → `:id` + leading slash. Detectors kept self-contained per the per-spec convention (`firstOfType`/normalize duplicated ~4× — filed a cleanup bead to extract shared helpers). Lesson: when extending a hook to many unfamiliar grammars, a 10-line CST dumper pays for itself by the second language; and one well-shaped interface (`collectRoutes(root, rel) → {nodes, edges}`) cleanly absorbs call-registration, decorators, annotations, attribute-macros, and URL-tables alike. ama-a2r DONE (Go/PHP/Java/Rust/Django). 640 → 648 tests; suite + self-index + typecheck + lint green. Parity epic ama-514 — frameworks follow-up closed.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer/prisma · **Field-level Prisma linkage by *reusing* the `prisma-ref` provenance with a second target prefix — zero `EdgeProvenance` union churn** (ama-bgu) — `prisma.user.findMany({ where: { email, name }, select: { id: true }, orderBy: { createdAt: "desc" } })` now links to the `User.email`/`User.name`/`User.id`/`User.createdAt` Property nodes, so `impact_analysis(Model.field)` reaches the querying code. The model-ref work (ama-kvv) tagged `prisma.<model>` as a `prisma:model:<name>` candidate (provenance `prisma-ref`) resolved whole-graph to the model Class; for fields I reused the **same provenance** with a second prefix `prisma:field:<model>.<field>` and taught the **one** deriver (`derivePrismaReferences`) to resolve both (field prefix → Property by `qualifiedName`, model prefix → Class by name). Why it matters: a brand-new edge kind/provenance would have expanded the `EdgeProvenance` union → forced edits to `getGraphSchema`'s exhaustive record *and* the edge-provenance exact-match test (the exact churn the original Prisma work hit), and reusing the provenance also means the indexer's `relinkPrisma` needs **zero** changes — it already calls the deriver. Extraction: from the `prisma.<model>` access, walk up to the enclosing `.method(arg)` call's first object-literal arg and take the keys of its where/select/data/orderBy/… clauses (skipping `AND`/`OR`/`NOT`); a `ShorthandPropertyAssignment` (`{ email }`) exposes `.name` just like a `PropertyAssignment`, so `where: { email }` resolves. Lesson: when a feature is a *new flavor of an existing derived edge*, encode the flavor in the candidate target string (a prefix) — not a new provenance/edge-kind — for one resolver and no schema/union ripple. ama-bgu done (Prisma epic ama-apy). 648 → 650 tests; suite + self-index + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · api/parity · **The programmatic API already existed — `AmaSession` is a deliberately transport-free facade; `ama-dah` was packaging, not logic.** a comparable tool ships a an embeddable class; Ama's equivalent was already `AmaSession` (src/mcp/session.ts), whose own doc says *"Kept transport-free so it is unit-testable without standing up a stdio server"* — i.e. designed as a usable facade with every query method (searchSymbol/findCallers/impactAnalysis/explore/indexStatus/reindexFile/watch/close). So "export the query surface as a library" needed no new behavior: a ~50-line `src/api.ts` re-exports `AmaSession` (+ an `Ama` alias + `index()`/`open()` one-call entries that `new` + `indexRepository`/`open`) plus the graph/query result types, and `package.json` gained `main`/`types`/`exports` pointing at the built `dist/api.{js,d.ts}` (`declaration` was already on). The verification detail that matters for a *library*: I imported the **built** `dist/api.js` (exports `Ama, AmaSession, index, open`), not just the source the suite runs — because the `exports` map only resolves against `dist`, so a green suite alone wouldn't catch a broken package surface. Lesson: before building a "new capability", check whether an internal class is already the capability minus its packaging — a transport-free core (no stdio/JSON-RPC coupling baked in) is one re-export from being a public library, and the real work is the package surface (`exports`/`types`/`declaration`) verified against the build output. ama-dah done; parity epic ama-514 now 5/6 (only language breadth ama-4op left). 650 → 652 tests; suite + typecheck + lint green; built entry resolves.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer/python · **Baseline-tier heuristic call edges — Python within-file resolution; the keystone fix for the real battle-test feedback** (ama-bnj, slice 1) — a real cross-repo Python/FastAPI battle-test reported empty `find_callers`/`find_callees`/`blastRadius` and `affected(testsOnly) → []`. Root cause: baseline tier = *symbols only, no Calls edges*, and all the call/impact queries are deep-tier (TS-only). Slice 1 adds a `collectCalls` hook to `LanguageSpec` (parallel to `collectRoutes`); `pythonCalls` resolves each call's *simple* callee name (`foo()`/`obj.bar()` → `foo`/`bar`) to a function/method defined in the **same file** (via `qualifiedNameOf`, matching `walkSymbols`' nesting so ids line up) and emits a `Calls` edge from the enclosing function. Name-based, no types — so the honest guardrails: a name defined more than once in the file is left **unresolved** (mapped to `null`) rather than guess; module-level calls (no enclosing fn) are skipped; self-recursion + duplicate call sites are deduped. This makes callers/callees/blastRadius **non-empty for same-module relationships** (the user's "empty even for helper functions", for same-file helpers), reported honestly at tier `baseline`. Cross-file — what `affected` actually needs (tests import the module) — is slice 2: an import-guided whole-graph deriver, addable *without reworking this* (the analyzer keeps emitting within-file Calls; the deriver adds cross-file alongside). Lesson: "deep TS, baseline everything else" left non-TS users with empty impact analysis; a per-spec `collectCalls` hook + same-file name resolution is a cheap, honest first step that turns empty call queries into useful ones. ama-bnj slice 1 (within-file) done; cross-file deriver next. 652 → 655 tests; suite + self-index + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · query/feedback · **Exact-symbol search mode — opt-in, still index-backed** (ama-8sx, battle-test feedback) — searching `NotificationEmitter.emit` returned word-split substring noise (e.g. it matches the `emit` in `Emitter`) plus a low-confidence warning. Added `SearchOptions.exact`: seed candidates from the name index by the **last dotted segment** (`emit`) — so it stays index-backed rather than an `allNodes()` scan — then keep only nodes whose `name` **or** `qualifiedName` equals the full query (case-insensitive). Exposed as an `exact` boolean on the `search_symbol` MCP tool. Lesson: an "exact" mode over a substring/FTS index isn't "skip the index" — it's "use the index to get candidates, then tighten the predicate"; searching by the simple name and filtering to the full dotted match keeps a precise `Foo.bar` lookup off the whole-graph scan. ama-8sx done. 655 → 657 tests; suite + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer/python · **Cross-file Python call resolution — an import-guided whole-graph deriver completes the keystone** (ama-bnj slice 2) — slice 1 resolved same-file calls in the analyzer; slice 2 closes the loop for `affected` (tests live in separate files). The analyzer now emits a `call:<name>` candidate (`References`/`call-ref`) for every non-local, non-builtin call; `deriveCallEdges` resolves it whole-graph — find a function named `<name>` in a file the caller's file **imports** (via the File→File `Imports` edges) → a `Calls` edge (provenance `call`). The indexer's `relinkCalls` (a mirror of `relinkPrisma`/`redispatch`) recomputes them after every index/reindex. It reuses the prisma candidate→deriver→provenance shape exactly: a `call-ref` candidate that persists (identified by its `call:` target prefix) + a `call` resolved edge recomputed via `replaceEdgesByProvenance`. Import-guided resolution is **precise** (the import names the file) and name-based only within it — far better than a global same-name match — and a builtins skip-set keeps candidate noise down. Two side-lessons: (1) expanding the `EdgeProvenance` union forced the usual `getGraphSchema` record + edge-provenance test updates — the standing tax for a new provenance; (2) chasing why an Edit kept "not matching" surfaced a stray **raw NULL byte** in slice 1's dedup key — ` ` had become a literal `\0`, which makes `grep` treat the file as binary — and it turned out I was *matching* the TS analyzer's own convention (`analyzer.ts` uses `\0` key separators); fixed Python to a space and filed a cleanup for the pre-existing ones. Net for the battle-test: callers/callees/blastRadius/`affected` now reach across imported modules for Python/FastAPI. ama-bnj DONE (within + cross-file). 655 → 660 tests; suite + self-index + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · indexer/feedback · **`IGNORED_DIRS` only listed JS build dirs; non-dot vendored dirs flooded a parent-workspace index** (ama-65z) — battle-test feedback: pointing Ama at a parent-of-repos pulled in duplicate/noisy cross-repo paths. The obvious guess (`.venv`) was *wrong*: `isIgnoredSegment` already skips anything `startsWith(".")`, so dot-dirs (`.venv`/`.git`/`.tox`/`.mypy_cache`) were handled. The real gap was the **non-dot** conventions — a plain `venv`, Python's `__pycache__`/`site-packages`, Rust/Maven `target`, Go/PHP `vendor` — none first-party source, all walked because they're neither dotted nor in the set. `site-packages` is the killer: thousands of third-party files, duplicated across every sub-repo's virtualenv → the "duplicate cross-repo paths + noise." Added them to `IGNORED_DIRS`. Lesson: a built-in ignore list that only names the JS ecosystem silently over-indexes other ecosystems; pair the leading-dot skip with an explicit non-dot vendor/build/cache list — and check the actual skip logic before assuming the obvious dir is the culprit (`.venv` was a red herring). Quick win shipped; the deeper parent-of-repos angle (nested `.gitignore` applied mid-walk, symlink/duplicate paths) still wants the reporter's layout. 660 → 663 tests; suite + self-index + typecheck + lint green.
- 2026-06-22 · analyzer/feedback · **FastAPI test-impact: bridge the test→handler gap via the route path the test names** (ama-f2c) — both battle-test rounds reported `affected(testsOnly)` empty for FastAPI even on 0.2.0. Why: tests exercise routes through a `TestClient` (`client.get("/users/1")`) — there's *no direct call* to the handler, so the call graph (ama-bnj) couldn't reach them. Fix: the Python call detector now recognizes `<obj>.<verb>("/path")` (verb ∈ HTTP methods, path starts with `/`) and emits a `route:<METHOD> /concrete-path` candidate (reusing the `call-ref` channel, disambiguated by the `route:` vs `call:` prefix); `deriveRouteTestEdges` resolves it to the Route node by **param-aware path matching** (concrete `/users/1` ↔ pattern `/users/:id`, segment-wise with `:param`/`*` wildcards; an `ANY` route matches any verb) and emits a `test → route` `References` edge (provenance `route-test`); `relinkRouteTests` recomputes it whole-graph (mirror of `relinkCalls`). The chain works because `impactAnalysis` reverse-traverses `References` (it's in `IMPACT_EDGE_KINDS`): `impact(handler)` ← `route→handler` (existing) ← `test→route` (new) = reaches the test. Verified end-to-end. **Key design choice: link `test → route`, not `test → handler` — the existing `route→handler` edge does the last hop, so it's one new edge type and the route stays the join point.** Lesson: when a test exercises code *indirectly* (HTTP / DI / reflection) the static call graph can't see it — bridge through the protocol artifact the test *does* name (here, the URL path) plus the framework's own route→handler link. ama-f2c done (the FastAPI test-impact fix from two feedback rounds). 663 → 666 tests; suite + self-index + typecheck + lint green.
